			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Nuclear power</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">"Atomic power" and "Atomic Power" redirect here. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Power_(film)" title="Atomic Power (film)">Atomic Power (film)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about nuclear fission and fusion power sources primarily. For commercial quantities of nuclear energy attained from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a>. For the political term, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">List of states with nuclear weapons</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/220px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/330px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/440px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3328" data-file-height="2377" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1200 <a href="/wiki/MWe" class="mw-redirect" title="MWe">MWe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leibstadt_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant">Leibstadt fission-electric power station</a> in Switzerland. The <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactor</a> (BWR), located inside the dome capped cylindrical structure, is dwarfed in size by its <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling tower</a>. The station produces a yearly average of 25 million <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hours</a> per day, sufficient to power a city the size of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/220px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/330px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/440px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Palo_Verde_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station">Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the largest in the US with 3 <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactors</a>(PWRs), is situated in the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Arizona desert">Arizona desert</a>. It uses <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> from cities as its <a href="/wiki/Cooling_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooling water">cooling water</a> in 9 squat mechanical draft cooling towers.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Its total <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Spent fuel">spent fuel</a>/"waste" inventory produced since 1986, is contained in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> cylinders located between the artificial body of water and the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_switchyard" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical switchyard">electrical switchyard</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TaskForce_One.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/TaskForce_One.jpg/220px-TaskForce_One.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/TaskForce_One.jpg/330px-TaskForce_One.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/TaskForce_One.jpg/440px-TaskForce_One.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1062" data-file-height="786" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TaskForce_One.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. nuclear powered ships,(top to bottom) cruisers <a href="/wiki/USS_Bainbridge_(CGN-25)" title="USS Bainbridge (CGN-25)">USS <i>Bainbridge</i></a>, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)" title="USS Long Beach (CGN-9)">USS <i>Long Beach</i></a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)">USS Enterprise</a></i>, the first nuclear-powered <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a>. Picture taken in 1964 during a record setting voyage of 26,540 nmi (49,190 km) around the world in 65 days without refueling. Crew members are spelling out <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass-energy equivalence</a> formula <i>E&#160;=&#160;mc<sup>2</sup></i> on the flight deck.</div>

<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00CC4B; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Others (<a href="/wiki/Renewables" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewables">Renew.</a>) (5.0%)</div>

</div>
<p><b>Nuclear power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> that release <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear energy</a><sup id="cite_ref-nuclearEnergy-tx_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclearEnergy-tx-5">[5]</a></sup> to generate heat, which most frequently is then used in <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a> to produce electricity in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. The term includes <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. Presently, the nuclear fission of elements in the <a href="/wiki/Actinide#Applications" title="Actinide">actinide</a> series of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> produce the vast majority of nuclear energy in the direct service of humankind, with nuclear decay processes, primarily in the form of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>, in niche uses making up the rest.</p>
<p>Owing, fundamentally, to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_physics" title="Nuclear reactor physics">delayed critical</a> fission process, Fission-electric <a href="/wiki/Power_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Power stations">power stations</a> are one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> methods of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, and in terms of <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">total life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions per unit of energy generated</a>, has emission values lower than "<a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>" when the latter is taken as a single energy source.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Since all electricity supplying technologies use <a href="/wiki/Cement#CO2_emissions" title="Cement">cement</a>, etc., during construction, emissions are yet to be brought to zero. A 2014 analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> literature by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) reported that the embodied <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle assessment">total life-cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> of fission electricity has a <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> value of 12 g CO<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">eq</a>/<a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a> which is the lowest out of all commercial <a href="/wiki/Baseload" class="mw-redirect" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy sources,<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-9">[9]</a></sup> and second lowest out of all commercial electricity technologies known, after <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> which is an <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_energy_source" title="Intermittent energy source">Intermittent energy source</a> with embodied greenhouse gas emissions, per unit of energy generated of 11 g CO<sub>2</sub>eq/kWh. Each result is contrasted with coal and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil gas">fossil gas</a> at 820 and 490 g CO<sub>2</sub> eq/kWh.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-9">[9]</a></sup> With this translating into, from the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear power station">fission-electric power station</a> commercialization in the 1970s, having prevented the emission of about 64 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a>, greenhouse gases that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of fossil fuels in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a social <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">debate about nuclear power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power-13">[13]</a></sup> Proponents, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmentalists_for_Nuclear_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy">Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy</a>, contend that nuclear power is a safe, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Opponents</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenpeace International">Greenpeace International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Information_and_Resource_Service" title="Nuclear Information and Resource Service">NIRS</a>, contend that nuclear power poses many threats to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity" title="Environmental radioactivity">people and the environment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Share_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Share-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_WARN_.C2.BB_Nuclear_Power_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC_WARN_.C2.BB_Nuclear_Power-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sturgis_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">fission power reactor accidents</a>, or accidents that resulted in medium to long-lived <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission product</a> contamination of inhabited areas, have occurred in <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">Generation I and II reactor</a> designs, blueprinted between 1950 and 1980. These include the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> which occurred in 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> (2011), and the more contained <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> (1979).<sup id="cite_ref-timenuke_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-18">[18]</a></sup> There have also been some nuclear submarine accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-timenuke_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rad_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rad-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnston2007_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-20">[20]</a></sup> In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, analysis has determined that fission-electric reactors have caused fewer fatalities per unit of energy generated than the other major sources of energy generation. Energy production from coal, petroleum, natural gas and hydroelectricity has caused a greater number of fatalities per unit of energy generated due to air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accident</a> effects.<sup id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gohlke_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gohlke-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theage2006_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-26">[26]</a></sup> Four years after the Fukushima-Daiichi accident, there have been no fatalities due to exposure to radiation, and no discernible increased incidence of radiation-related health effects are expected among exposed members of the public and their descendants.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Japan Times estimated 1,600 deaths were the result of evacuation, due to physical and mental stress stemming from long stays at shelters, a lack of initial care as a result of hospitals being disabled by the <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>, and suicides.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015:<sup id="cite_ref-MyUser_Https:_May_22_2016c_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MyUser_Https:_May_22_2016c-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Ten new reactors were connected to the grid.</li>
<li>Seven reactors were permanently shut down.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">441 reactors</a> had a worldwide net capacity of 382,855 megawatts of electricity.</li>
<li>67 new nuclear reactors were under construction.</li>
</ul>
<p>Most of the new activity is in China where there is an urgent need to control pollution from coal plants.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2016, <a href="/wiki/Watts_Bar_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Watts Bar Nuclear Generating Station">Watts Bar 2</a> became the first new United States reactor to enter commercial operation since 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission#History" title="Nuclear fission">Nuclear fission §&#160;History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Age" title="Atomic Age">Atomic Age</a></div>
<p>In 1932 physicist <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> discovered that when lithium atoms were "split" by protons from a proton accelerator, immense amounts of energy were released in accordance with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">mass–energy equivalence</a>. However, he and other nuclear physics pioneers <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> believed harnessing the power of the atom for practical purposes anytime in the near future was unlikely, with Rutherford labeling such expectations "moonshine."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same year, his doctoral student <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> which was immediately recognized as a potential tool for nuclear experimentation because of its lack of an electric charge. Experimentation with bombardment of materials with neutrons led <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a> to discover <a href="/wiki/Induced_radioactivity" title="Induced radioactivity">induced radioactivity</a> in 1934, which allowed the creation of radium-like elements at much less the price of natural radium.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Further work by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> in the 1930s focused on using <a href="/wiki/Slow_neutron" class="mw-redirect" title="Slow neutron">slow neutrons</a> to increase the effectiveness of induced radioactivity. Experiments bombarding uranium with neutrons led Fermi to believe he had created a new, transuranic element, which was dubbed <a href="/wiki/Hesperium" title="Hesperium">hesperium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Np_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Np-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg/220px-First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg/330px-First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg/440px-First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_nuclear_chain_reaction.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
December 2, 1942. A depiction of the scene when scientists observed the world's first man made nuclear reactor, the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, as it became self-sustaining/critical at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>But in 1938, German chemists <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a>, along with Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and Meitner's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Robert_Frisch" title="Otto Robert Frisch">Otto Robert Frisch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> conducted experiments with the products of neutron-bombarded uranium, as a means of further investigating Fermi's claims. They determined that the relatively tiny neutron split the nucleus of the massive uranium atoms into two roughly equal pieces, contradicting Fermi.<sup id="cite_ref-Np_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Np-35">[35]</a></sup> This was an extremely surprising result: all other forms of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> involved only small changes to the mass of the nucleus, whereas this process—dubbed "fission" as a <a href="/wiki/Binary_fission" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary fission">reference to biology</a>—involved a complete rupture of the nucleus. Numerous scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" class="mw-redirect" title="Leó Szilárd">Leó Szilárd</a>, who was one of the first, recognized that if fission reactions released additional neutrons, a self-sustaining <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> could result. Once this was experimentally confirmed and announced by Frédéric Joliot-Curie in 1939, scientists in many countries (including the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, and the Soviet Union) petitioned their governments for support of nuclear fission research, just on the cusp of World War II, for the development of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, where Fermi and Szilárd had both emigrated, this led to the creation of the first man-made reactor, known as <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, which achieved <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">criticality</a> on December 2, 1942. This work became part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, which made <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> and built large reactors to breed <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> for use in the first nuclear weapons, which were <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">used on the cities of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/220px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/330px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/440px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="373" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first light bulbs ever lit by electricity generated by nuclear power at <a href="/wiki/EBR-1" class="mw-redirect" title="EBR-1">EBR-1</a> at <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory-West</a>, December 20, 1951.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1945, the pocketbook <i>The Atomic Age</i> heralded the untapped atomic power in everyday objects and depicted a future where fossil fuels would go unused. One science writer, David Dietz, wrote that instead of filling the gas tank of a car two or three times a week, people will travel for a year on a pellet of atomic energy the size of a vitamin pill. <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Seaborg" class="mw-redirect" title="Glenn Seaborg">Glenn Seaborg</a>, who chaired the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a>, wrote "there will be nuclear powered earth-to-moon shuttles, nuclear powered artificial hearts, plutonium heated swimming pools for SCUBA divers, and much more". These optimistic predications remain unfulfilled.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United Kingdom, Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and the USSR proceeded over the course of the late 1940s and early 1950s. Electricity was generated for the first time by a nuclear reactor on December 20, 1951, at the <a href="/wiki/EBR-I" class="mw-redirect" title="EBR-I">EBR-I</a> experimental station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, which initially produced about 100&#160;kW.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Work was also strongly researched in the US on nuclear marine propulsion, with a test reactor being developed by 1953 (eventually, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS Nautilus</a>, the first nuclear-powered submarine, would launch in 1955).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1953, US President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> gave his "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>" speech at the United Nations, emphasizing the need to develop "peaceful" uses of nuclear power quickly. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1954" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1954">1954 Amendments to the Atomic Energy Act</a> which allowed rapid declassification of US reactor technology and encouraged development by the private sector.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h3>
<p>On June 27, 1954, the <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant</a> became the world's first nuclear power plant to generate electricity for a <a href="/wiki/Power_grid" class="mw-redirect" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, and produced around 5 megawatts of electric power.<sup id="cite_ref-IAEANews_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEANews-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WNA_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later in 1954, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Strauss" title="Lewis Strauss">Lewis Strauss</a>, then chairman of the United States Atomic Energy Commission (U.S. AEC, forerunner of the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a>) spoke of electricity in the future being "<a href="/wiki/Too_cheap_to_meter" title="Too cheap to meter">too cheap to meter</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-thisdayinquotes_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thisdayinquotes-47">[47]</a></sup> Strauss was very likely referring to hydrogen fusion<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> —which was secretly being developed as part of <a href="/wiki/Project_Sherwood" title="Project Sherwood">Project Sherwood</a> at the time—but Strauss's statement was interpreted as a promise of very cheap energy from nuclear fission. The U.S. AEC itself had issued far more realistic testimony regarding nuclear fission to the U.S. Congress only months before, projecting that "costs can be brought down... [to]... about the same as the cost of electricity from conventional sources..."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955 the United Nations' "First Geneva Conference", then the world's largest gathering of scientists and engineers, met to explore the technology. In 1957 <a href="/wiki/EURATOM" class="mw-redirect" title="EURATOM">EURATOM</a> was launched alongside the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (the latter is now the European Union). The same year also saw the launch of the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HD.15.019_(11823864155).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg/220px-HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg/330px-HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg/440px-HD.15.019_%2811823864155%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2558" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HD.15.019_(11823864155).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calder Hall, United Kingdom - The world's first commercial nuclear power station. First connected to the national power grid on 27 August 1956 and officially opened by Queen Elizabeth II on 17 October 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/220px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/330px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/440px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="662" data-file-height="516" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">Shippingport Atomic Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shippingport,_Pennsylvania" title="Shippingport, Pennsylvania">Shippingport, Pennsylvania</a> was the first commercial reactor in the USA and was opened in 1957.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The world's first commercial nuclear power station, <a href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall</a> at Windscale, England, was opened in 1956 with an initial capacity of 50 MW (later 200 MW).<sup id="cite_ref-Kragh_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kragh-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc17oct_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc17oct-51">[51]</a></sup> The first commercial nuclear generator to become operational in the United States was the <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Shippingport Reactor">Shippingport Reactor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, December 1957).</p>
<p>One of the first organizations to develop nuclear power was the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a>, for the purpose of propelling <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. The first nuclear-powered submarine, <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS&#160;<i>Nautilus</i>&#160;(SSN-571)</a>, was put to sea in December 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-52">[52]</a></sup> As of 2016, the U.S. Navy submarine fleet is made up entirely of nuclear-powered vessels, with 75 submarines in service. Two U.S. nuclear submarines, <a href="/wiki/USS_Scorpion_(SSN-589)" title="USS Scorpion (SSN-589)">USS&#160;<i>Scorpion</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Thresher_(SSN-593)" title="USS Thresher (SSN-593)">USS&#160;<i>Thresher</i></a>, have been lost at sea. The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Navy" title="Russian Navy">Russian Navy</a> is currently (2016) estimated to have 61 nuclear submarines in service; eight Soviet and Russian nuclear submarines have been lost at sea. This includes the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-19" title="Soviet submarine K-19">Soviet submarine K-19</a> reactor accident in 1961 which resulted in 8 deaths and more than 30 other people were over-exposed to radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-rad_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rad-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">Soviet submarine K-27</a> reactor accident in 1968 resulted in 9 fatalities and 83 other injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-johnston2007_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-20">[20]</a></sup> Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-429" title="Soviet submarine K-429">Soviet submarine K-429</a> sank twice, but was raised after each incident. Several <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">serious nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have involved nuclear submarine mishaps.<sup id="cite_ref-timenuke_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnston2007_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> also had a <a href="/wiki/Army_Nuclear_Power_Program" title="Army Nuclear Power Program">nuclear power program</a>, beginning in 1954. The SM-1 Nuclear Power Plant, at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Belvoir" title="Fort Belvoir">Fort Belvoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, was the first power reactor in the U.S. to supply electrical energy to a commercial grid (VEPCO), in April 1957, before Shippingport. The <a href="/wiki/SL-1" title="SL-1">SL-1</a> was a U.S. Army experimental nuclear power reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">National Reactor Testing Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Idaho" title="Eastern Idaho">eastern</a> <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>. It underwent a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">meltdown</a> in January 1961, which killed its three operators.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In the Soviet Union at The <a href="/wiki/Mayak" title="Mayak">Mayak</a> Production Association facility there were a number of accidents, including an explosion, that released 50-100 tonnes of high-level radioactive waste, contaminating a huge territory in the eastern Urals and causing numerous deaths and injuries. The Soviet regime kept this accident secret for about 30 years. The event was eventually rated at 6 on the seven-level INES scale (third in severity only to the disasters at <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl" title="Chernobyl">Chernobyl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima nuclear accident">Fukushima</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The status of nuclear power globally<br />
(click image for legend)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/220px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/330px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/440px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Washington_Public_Power_Supply_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Public Power Supply System">Washington Public Power Supply System</a> Nuclear Power Plants 3 and 5 were never completed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Installed nuclear capacity initially rose relatively quickly, rising from less than 1 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW) in 1960 to 100 GW in the late 1970s, and 300 GW in the late 1980s. Since the late 1980s worldwide capacity has risen much more slowly, reaching 366 GW in 2005. Between around 1970 and 1990, more than 50 GW of capacity was under construction (peaking at over 150 GW in the late 1970s and early 1980s) — in 2005, around 25 GW of new capacity was planned. More than two-thirds of all nuclear plants ordered after January 1970 were eventually cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-52">[52]</a></sup> A total of <a href="/wiki/List_of_canceled_nuclear_plants_in_the_United_States" title="List of canceled nuclear plants in the United States">63 nuclear units were canceled</a> in the USA between 1975 and 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1970s and 1980s rising economic costs (related to extended construction times largely due to regulatory changes and pressure-group litigation)<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and falling fossil fuel prices made nuclear power plants then under construction less attractive. In the 1980s (U.S.) and 1990s (Europe), flat load growth and <a href="/wiki/Electricity_liberalization" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity liberalization">electricity liberalization</a> also made the addition of large new baseload capacity unattractive.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> had a significant effect on countries, such as France and Japan, which had relied more heavily on oil for electric generation (39%<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and 73% respectively) to invest in nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some local opposition to nuclear power emerged in the early 1960s,<sup id="cite_ref-well_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-well-58">[58]</a></sup> and in the late 1960s some members of the scientific community began to express their concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-wolfgang_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-59">[59]</a></sup> These concerns related to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">high cost of nuclear power plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive waste disposal">radioactive waste disposal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bm_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bm-60">[60]</a></sup> In the early 1970s, there were large protests about a proposed nuclear power plant in <a href="/wiki/Wyhl" title="Wyhl">Wyhl</a>, Germany. The project was cancelled in 1975 and anti-nuclear success at Wyhl inspired opposition to nuclear power in other parts of Europe and North America.<sup id="cite_ref-pub_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pub-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-got_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-got-62">[62]</a></sup> By the mid-1970s anti-nuclear activism had moved beyond local protests and politics to gain a wider appeal and influence, and nuclear power became an issue of major public protest.<sup id="cite_ref-jimfalk_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jimfalk-63">[63]</a></sup> Although it lacked a single co-ordinating organization, and did not have uniform goals, the movement's efforts gained a great deal of attention.<sup id="cite_ref-eleven_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-64">[64]</a></sup> In some countries, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">nuclear power conflict</a> "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies".<sup id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ANTIAKW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/220px-ANTIAKW.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/330px-ANTIAKW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/440px-ANTIAKW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2232" data-file-height="1416" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ANTIAKW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
120,000 people attended an <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_protest" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-nuclear protest">anti-nuclear protest</a> in Bonn, Germany, on October 14, 1979, following the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kits_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kits-66">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In France, between 1975 and 1977, some 175,000 people protested against nuclear power in ten demonstrations.<sup id="cite_ref-kits_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kits-66">[66]</a></sup> In West Germany, between February 1975 and April 1979, some 280,000 people were involved in seven demonstrations at nuclear sites. Several site occupations were also attempted. In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979, some 120,000 people attended a demonstration against nuclear power in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kits_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kits-66">[66]</a></sup> In May 1979, an estimated 70,000 people, including then governor of California <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a>, attended a march and rally against nuclear power in Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-diablo_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diablo-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_anti-nuclear_power_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="List of anti-nuclear power groups">Anti-nuclear power groups</a> emerged in every country that has had a nuclear power programme.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Mile_Island_and_Chernobyl">Three Mile Island and Chernobyl</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/220px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/330px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/440px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2240" data-file-height="1680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The abandoned city of <a href="/wiki/Pripyat_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pripyat (city)">Pripyat</a> with Chernobyl plant in the distance.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Health and safety concerns, the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">accident at Three Mile Island</a>, and the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> played a part in stopping new plant construction in many countries,<sup id="cite_ref-wolfgang_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-59">[59]</a></sup> although the public policy organization, the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> states that new nuclear units, at the time of publishing in 2006, had not been built in the U.S. because of soft demand for electricity, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_overrun" title="Cost overrun">cost overruns</a> on nuclear plants due to regulatory issues and construction delays.<sup id="cite_ref-tbi_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tbi-68">[68]</a></sup> By the end of the 1970s it became clear that nuclear power would not grow nearly as dramatically as once believed. Eventually, more than 120 reactor orders in the U.S. were ultimately cancelled<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and the construction of new reactors ground to a halt. A cover story in the February 11, 1985, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine commented on the overall failure of the U.S. nuclear power program, saying it "ranks as the largest managerial disaster in business history".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike the Three Mile Island accident, the much more serious Chernobyl accident did not increase regulations affecting Western reactors since the Chernobyl reactors were of the problematic <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> design only used in the Soviet Union, for example lacking "robust" <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment buildings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NRC_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-71">[71]</a></sup> Many of these RBMK reactors are still in use today. However, changes were made in both the reactors themselves (use of a safer enrichment of uranium) and in the control system (prevention of disabling safety systems), amongst other things, to reduce the possibility of a duplicate accident.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>An international organization to promote safety awareness and professional development on operators in nuclear facilities was created: <a href="/wiki/World_Association_of_Nuclear_Operators" title="World Association of Nuclear Operators">WANO</a>; World Association of Nuclear Operators.</p>
<p>Opposition in Ireland and Poland prevented nuclear programs there, while Austria (1978), Sweden (1980) and Italy (1987) (influenced by Chernobyl) voted in referendums to oppose or phase out nuclear power. In July 2009, the Italian Parliament passed a law that cancelled the results of an earlier referendum and allowed the immediate start of the Italian nuclear program.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> a one-year moratorium was placed on nuclear power development,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> followed by a referendum in which over 94% of voters (turnout 57%) rejected plans for new nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/250px-OL3.jpg" width="250" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/375px-OL3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/500px-OL3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2813" data-file-height="1320" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_3" class="mw-redirect" title="Olkiluoto 3">Olkiluoto 3</a> under construction in 2009. It is the first <a href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="European Pressurized Reactor">EPR</a> design, but problems with workmanship and supervision have created costly delays which led to an inquiry by the Finnish nuclear regulator <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A4teilyturvakeskus" class="mw-redirect" title="Säteilyturvakeskus">STUK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wnn161009_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wnn161009-76">[76]</a></sup> In December 2012, Areva estimated that the full cost of building the reactor will be about €8.5 billion, or almost three times the original delivery price of €3 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WNN-2012-07-17_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNN-2012-07-17-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-2012-07-16_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-2012-07-16-79">[79]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png/250px-Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png" width="250" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png/375px-Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png/500px-Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png 2x" data-file-width="1288" data-file-height="732" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Electricity_supplied_from_nuclear_reactors_connected_to_the_grid.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Production of nuclear power plants<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</a></div>
<p>Since about 2001 the term <i>nuclear renaissance</i> has been used to refer to a possible nuclear power industry revival, driven by rising <a href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" class="mw-redirect" title="Price of petroleum">fossil fuel prices</a> and new concerns about meeting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emission limits.<sup id="cite_ref-intro_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intro-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> reported that nuclear electricity generation was at its lowest level since 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-wna13_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-82">[82]</a></sup> As of January 2016, however, 65 new nuclear power reactors were under construction. Over 150 were planned, equivalent to nearly half of capacity at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a></div>
<p>Japan's 2011 Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident, which occurred in a reactor design from the 1960s, prompted a re-examination of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">nuclear energy policy</a> in many countries.<sup id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-84">[84]</a></sup> Germany plans to close all its reactors by 2022, and Italy has re-affirmed its ban on electric utilities generating, but not importing, fission derived electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> halved its prior estimate of new generating capacity to be built by 2035.<sup id="cite_ref-economist-20110428_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-20110428-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-late_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-late-86">[86]</a></sup> In 2013 Japan signed a deal worth $22 billion, in which <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> would build four modern <i><a href="/wiki/Atmea" title="Atmea">Atmea</a></i> reactors for Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In August 2015, following 4 years of <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ci_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ōi Nuclear Power Plant">near zero</a> fission-electricity generation, Japan began restarting its <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Japan" title="Nuclear power in Japan">fission fleet</a>, after <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster#Equipment.2C_facility_and_operational_changes" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">safety upgrades were completed</a>, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Sendai_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Sendai Nuclear Power Plant">Sendai fission-electric station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2011 the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Fukushima_nuclear_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the Fukushima nuclear accidents">nuclear emergencies</a> at Japan's <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a> and shutdowns at <a href="/wiki/2011_Japanese_nuclear_incidents" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Japanese nuclear incidents">other nuclear facilities</a> raised questions among some commentators over the future of the renaissance.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> China, Germany, Switzerland, Israel, Malaysia, Thailand, United Kingdom, Italy<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and the Philippines have reviewed their nuclear power programs. Indonesia and Vietnam still plan to build nuclear power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.xinhuanet.com_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.xinhuanet.com-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>The World Nuclear Association has said that "nuclear power generation suffered its biggest ever one-year fall through 2012 as the bulk of the Japanese fleet remained offline for a full calendar year". Data from the International Atomic Energy Agency showed that nuclear power plants globally produced 2346 TWh of electricity in 2012 – seven per cent less than in 2011. The figures illustrate the effects of a full year of 48 Japanese power reactors producing no power during the year. The permanent closure of eight reactor units in Germany was also a factor. Problems at Crystal River, Fort Calhoun and the two San Onofre units in the USA meant they produced no power for the full year, while in Belgium Doel 3 and Tihange 2 were out of action for six months. Compared to 2010, the nuclear industry produced 11% less electricity in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-wna13_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Fukushima_controversy">Post-Fukushima controversy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atom-Moratorium.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Atom-Moratorium.svg/220px-Atom-Moratorium.svg.png" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Atom-Moratorium.svg/330px-Atom-Moratorium.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Atom-Moratorium.svg/440px-Atom-Moratorium.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="555" data-file-height="750" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atom-Moratorium.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eight of the seventeen operating reactors in Germany were permanently shut down as part of <a href="/wiki/Energiewende_in_Germany" title="Energiewende in Germany">Germany's <i>Energiewende</i></a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident sparked controversy about the importance of the accident and its effect on nuclear's future. IAEA Director General Yukiya Amano said the Japanese nuclear accident "caused deep public anxiety throughout the world and damaged confidence in nuclear power",<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and the International Energy Agency halved its estimate of additional nuclear generating capacity to be built by 2035.<sup id="cite_ref-economist-20110428_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-20110428-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-late_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-late-86">[86]</a></sup> But by 2015, the Agency's outlook had become more promising. "Nuclear power is a critical element in limiting greenhouse gas emissions," the agency noted, and "the prospects for nuclear energy remain positive in the medium to long term despite a negative impact in some countries in the aftermath of the [Fukushima-Daiichi] accident...it is still the second-largest source worldwide of low-carbon electricity. And the 72 reactors under construction at the start of last year were the most in 25 years."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Though <a href="/wiki/Platts" class="mw-redirect" title="Platts">Platts</a> reported in 2011 that "the crisis at Japan's Fukushima nuclear plants has prompted leading energy-consuming countries to review the safety of their existing reactors and cast doubt on the speed and scale of planned expansions around the world",<sup id="cite_ref-Platts_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-101">[101]</a></sup> Progress Energy Chairman/CEO Bill Johnson made the observation that "Today there’s an even more compelling case that greater use of nuclear power is a vital part of a balanced energy strategy".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In 2011, <i>The Economist</i> opined that nuclear power "looks dangerous, unpopular, expensive and risky", and that "it is replaceable with relative ease and could be forgone with no huge structural shifts in the way the world works".<sup id="cite_ref-econ2011_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ2011-103">[103]</a></sup> Earth Institute Director Jeffrey Sachs disagreed, claiming combating climate change would require an expansion of nuclear power. "We won't meet the carbon targets if nuclear is taken off the table," he said. "We need to understand the scale of the challenge."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Investment banks were critical of nuclear soon after the accident. Many disputed their impartiality, however, due to significant investments in renewable energy, perceived by some as a valid alternative to nuclear. In early April 2011, analysts at Swiss-based investment bank UBS said: "At Fukushima, four reactors have been out of control for weeks, casting doubt on whether even an advanced economy can master nuclear safety...we believe the Fukushima accident was the most serious ever for the credibility of nuclear power".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> UBS has helped to raise more than $20 billion since 2006 and advised on more than a dozen deals for renewable energy and cleantech companies.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> advised that "the global impact of the Fukushima accident is a fundamental shift in public perception with regard to how a nation prioritizes and values its populations health, safety, security, and natural environment when determining its current and future energy pathways...renewable energy will be a clear long-term winner in most energy systems, a conclusion supported by many voter surveys conducted over the past few weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> has over €1 billion in capital invested in renewables projects in Europe, North &amp; South America, and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manufacturers also recognized a profit opportunity in negative public perceptions about nuclear. In September 2011, German engineering giant <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> announced it will withdraw entirely from the nuclear industry, as a response to the Fukushima nuclear accident in Japan, and said that it would no longer build nuclear power plants anywhere in the world. The company’s chairman, Peter Löscher, said that "Siemens was ending plans to cooperate with Rosatom, the Russian state-controlled nuclear power company, in the construction of dozens of nuclear plants throughout Russia over the coming two decades".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC18Sep2011_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC18Sep2011-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Renewable energy is a core component of Siemens's profit base. In February, 2016 the firm proposed a €10 billion renewable energy investment in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2012, the United States Nuclear Regulatory Commission approved the construction of two additional reactors at the <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a>, the first reactors to be approved in over 30 years since the Three Mile Island accident,<sup id="cite_ref-yah12_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yah12-112">[112]</a></sup> but NRC Chairman <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Jaczko" title="Gregory Jaczko">Gregory Jaczko</a> cast a dissenting vote citing safety concerns stemming from Japan's 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, and saying "I cannot support issuing this license as if Fukushima never happened".<sup id="cite_ref-us12_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us12-113">[113]</a></sup> Jaczko resigned in April 2012. One week after Southern received the license to begin major construction on the two new reactors, a dozen environmental and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> groups sued to stop the Plant Vogtle expansion project, saying "public safety and environmental problems since Japan's Fukushima Daiichi nuclear reactor accident have not been taken into account".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In July 2012, the suit was rejected by the Washington, D.C. Circuit Court of Appeals.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Countries such as <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement_in_Australia" title="Anti-nuclear movement in Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement_in_Austria" title="Anti-nuclear movement in Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Denmark" title="Nuclear energy in Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Greece" title="Nuclear energy in Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Ireland" title="Nuclear energy in Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear energy in Italy">Italy</a>, Latvia, Liechtenstein, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Luxembourg" title="Nuclear energy in Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Malta" title="Nuclear energy in Malta">Malta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Portugal" title="Nuclear energy in Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear power in Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Malaysia" title="Nuclear energy in Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand%27s_nuclear-free_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand's nuclear-free zone">New Zealand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_in_Norway" title="Nuclear energy in Norway">Norway</a> have no nuclear power reactors and remain opposed to nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-econ2011_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ2011-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gl2011_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gl2011-116">[116]</a></sup> However, by contrast, some countries remain in favor, and financially support nuclear fusion research, including EU wide funding of the <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> project.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Companies">Companies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_in_the_nuclear_sector" title="List of companies in the nuclear sector">List of companies in the nuclear sector</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">Nuclear power by country</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity_and_production">Capacity and production</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentage of a nations electricity, produced by fission-electric power stations.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/220px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png" width="220" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/330px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/440px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="967" data-file-height="528" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Net <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generation">electrical generation</a> by source and growth from 1980 to 2010. (Brown) - fossil fuels.(Red) - Fission.(Green)- "all renewables". In terms of energy generated between 1980 and 2010, the contribution from fission grew the fastest.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_history.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png" width="220" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The rate of new construction builds for civilian fission-electric reactors essentially halted in the late 1980s, with the effects of accidents having a <a href="/wiki/Chilling_effect" title="Chilling effect">chilling effect</a>. Increased <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> realizations in existing reactors was primarily responsible for the continuing increase in electrical energy produced during this period. The halting of new builds c. 1985, resulted in greater fossil fuel generation, see above graph.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/220px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png" width="220" height="145" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/330px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/440px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 2x" data-file-width="2050" data-file-height="1350" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Electricity generation trends in the top five fission-energy producing countries (US EIA data)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nuclear power capacity remained relatively stable between the mid 1980s until the accident at the Fukushima Daiichi reactor in March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In June 2015, Platts reported global nuclear generation increased by 1% in 2014, the first annual increase since Fukushima.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United States produces the most nuclear energy, with nuclear power providing 19%<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> of the electricity it consumes, while France produces the highest percentage of its electrical energy from nuclear reactors—80% as of 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-npr20060501_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr20060501-122">[122]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> as a whole, nuclear energy provides 30% of the electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a> differs among European Union countries, and some, such as Austria, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Ireland and Italy, have no active nuclear power stations. In comparison, France has a large number of these plants, with 16 multi-unit stations in current use.</p>
<p>Many military and some civilian (such as some <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreaker</a>) ships use <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear marine propulsion</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> A few space vehicles have been launched using full-fledged <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>: 33 reactors belong to the Soviet <a href="/wiki/RORSAT" class="mw-redirect" title="RORSAT">RORSAT</a> series and one was the American <a href="/wiki/SNAP-10A" title="SNAP-10A">SNAP-10A</a>.</p>
<p>International research is continuing into safety improvements such as <a href="/wiki/Passively_safe" class="mw-redirect" title="Passively safe">passively safe</a> plants,<sup id="cite_ref-David_Baurac_2002_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Baurac_2002-125">[125]</a></sup> the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>, and additional uses of process heat such as <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting#Nuclear-thermal" title="Water splitting">hydrogen production</a> (in support of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>), for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalinating</a> sea water, and for use in <a href="/wiki/District_heating" title="District heating">district heating</a> systems.</p>
<p>Nuclear (fission) power stations, excluding the contribution from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">naval nuclear fission reactors</a>, provided 11% of the world's electricity in 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-oecd_pdf_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecd_pdf-126">[126]</a></sup> somewhat less than that generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydro-electric stations</a> at 16%. Since electricity accounts for about 25% of humanity's energy usage with the majority of the rest coming from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> reliant sectors such as transport, manufacture and home heating, nuclear fission's contribution to the <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global final energy consumption</a> is about 2.5%,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> a little more than the combined global electricity production from "new renewables"; wind, solar, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> and geothermal power, which together provided 2% of global final energy consumption in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regional differences in the use of fission energy are large.
