Fission energy generation, with a 20% share of the U.S. electricity production, is the single largest deployed technology among current <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> sources in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-issues_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-issues-129">[129]</a></sup> In addition, two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s twenty-seven nations' low-carbon energy is produced by fission.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Some of these nations have banned its generation, such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Italy" title="Nuclear power in Italy">Italy</a>, which ended the use of fission-electric generation, which started in 1963, in 1990. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_France" title="Nuclear power in France">France is the largest user of nuclear energy, deriving 75% of its electricity from fission</a>.</p>
<p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a> reported that there were 437&#160;operational civil fission-electric reactors<sup id="cite_ref-iaea.org_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea.org-131">[131]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">31&#160;countries</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-UIC_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UIC-132">[132]</a></sup> although not every reactor was producing electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In addition, there were approximately 140 naval vessels using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a> in operation, powered by some 180 reactors.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> As of 2013, attaining a <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">net energy gain</a> from sustained nuclear fusion reactions, excluding natural fusion power sources such as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, remains an ongoing area of international <a href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Fusion_Materials_Irradiation_Facility" title="International Fusion Materials Irradiation Facility">engineering research</a>. With commercial fusion power production remaining <a href="/wiki/DEMO" title="DEMO">unlikely before 2050</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ITERorg_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since commercial nuclear energy began in the mid-1950s, 2008 was the first year that no new nuclear power plant was connected to the grid, although two were connected in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-tf2010_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tf2010-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/IAEA" class="mw-redirect" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> reported that worldwide there were 67 civil fission-electric power reactors under construction in 15 countries including <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Gulf states</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/APR-1400" title="APR-1400">United Arab Emirates</a> (UAE).<sup id="cite_ref-iaea.org_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea.org-131">[131]</a></sup> Over half of the 67 total being built were in Asia, with 28 in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Eight new grid connections were completed by China in 2015<sup id="cite_ref-WNN20160307_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNN20160307-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> and the most recently completed reactor to be connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>, as of January 2016, was at the <a href="/wiki/Kori_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Kori Nuclear Power Plant">Kori Nuclear Power Plant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Korea">Republic of Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NEIShinKori_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEIShinKori-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> In the US, four new <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> were under construction at <a href="/wiki/Vogtle" class="mw-redirect" title="Vogtle">Vogtle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgil_C._Summer_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station">Summer station</a>, while a fifth was nearing completion at <a href="/wiki/Watts_Bar_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Watts Bar Nuclear Generating Station">Watts Bar station</a>, all five were expected to become operational before 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-us12_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us12-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2013, four aging uncompetitive U.S reactors were closed.<sup id="cite_ref-Mark_Cooper_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_Cooper-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mw11111_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw11111-145">[145]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a>, the global trend is for new nuclear power stations coming online to be balanced by the number of old plants being retired.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analysis in 2015 by Professor and Chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation's building programs in the 1980s, within 10 years nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets.".<sup id="cite_ref-journals.plos.org_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-journals.plos.org-147">[147]</a></sup> In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a href="/wiki/Synfuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Synfuels">synfuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation's already proven decadal rates(in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>(GW/year/$).<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is in contrast to the completely conceptual paper-studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> more costly global investment per year, which has no historical precedent, having never been attempted due to its prohibitive cost,<sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-150">[150]</a></sup> along with far greater land that would need to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, and the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-151">[151]</a></sup> As Brook notes the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">Economics of new nuclear power plants</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg/220px-2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg/330px-2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg/440px-2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="343" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2005_Energy_Policy_Act.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> signing the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act of 2005</a>, which was designed to promote the US nuclear power industry, through incentives and subsidies, including cost-overrun support up to a total of $2 billion for six new nuclear plants.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Quiggin_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quiggin-152">[152]</a></sup> However, as of 2014 some electric utilities have rebuffed the loan package, including <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Electric_and_Gas" class="mw-redirect" title="South Carolina Electric and Gas">South Carolina Electric and Gas</a> which operates <a href="/wiki/Virgil_C._Summer_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station">Summer Station</a> (the location of 2 new builds), noting instead that "it was easier to raise [loan] money commercially."<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/220px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/330px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/440px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Ikata_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ikata Nuclear Power Plant">Ikata Nuclear Power Plant</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> that cools by utilizing a secondary coolant <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchanger</a> with a large body of water, an alternative cooling approach to large <a href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plants</a> typically have high capital costs for building the plant, but low fuel costs. Although nuclear power plants can vary their output the electricity is generally less favorably priced when doing so. Nuclear power plants are therefore typically run as much as possible to keep the cost of the generated electrical energy as low as possible, supplying mostly base-load electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Internationally the price of nuclear plants rose 15% annually in 1970-1990.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Yet, nuclear power has total costs in 2012 of about $96 per megawatt hour (MWh), most of which involves capital construction costs, compared with solar power at $130 per MWh, and natural gas at the low end at $64 per MWh.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, the <i><a href="/wiki/Bulletin_of_the_Atomic_Scientists" title="Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists">Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists</a></i> unveiled the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://thebulletin.org/introducing-nuclear-fuel-cycle-cost-calculator8361">Nuclear Fuel Cycle Cost Calculator</a>, an online tool that estimates the full cost of electricity produced by three configurations of the nuclear fuel cycle. Two years in the making, this interactive calculator is the first generally accessible model to provide a nuanced look at the economic costs of nuclear power; it lets users test how sensitive the price of electricity is to a full range of components—more than 60 parameters that can be adjusted for the three configurations of the nuclear fuel cycle considered by this tool (once-through, limited-recycle, full-recycle). Users can select the fuel cycle they would like to examine, change cost estimates for each component of that cycle, and even choose uncertainty ranges for the cost of particular components. This approach allows users around the world to compare the cost of different nuclear power approaches in a sophisticated way, while taking account of prices relevant to their own countries or regions.</p>
<p>In recent years there has been a slowdown of electricity demand growth.<sup id="cite_ref-kidd2011_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-157">[157]</a></sup> In Eastern Europe, a number of long-established projects are struggling to find finance, notably Belene in Bulgaria and the additional reactors at Cernavoda in Romania, and some potential backers have pulled out.<sup id="cite_ref-kidd2011_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-157">[157]</a></sup> Where the electricity market is competitive, cheap natural gas is available, and its future supply relatively secure, this also poses a major problem for nuclear projects<sup id="cite_ref-kidd2011_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-157">[157]</a></sup> and existing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT122214_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT122214-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties. To date all operating nuclear power plants were developed by <a href="/wiki/Nationalized" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalized">state-owned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">utility monopolies</a><sup id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-159">[159]</a></sup> where many of the risks associated with construction costs, operating performance, fuel price, accident liability and other factors were borne by consumers rather than suppliers. In addition, because the potential liability from a nuclear accident is so great, the full cost of liability insurance is generally limited/capped by the government, which the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission">U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> concluded constituted a significant subsidy.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Many countries have now liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the 2011 Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster, costs are expected to increase for currently operating and new nuclear power plants, due to increased requirements for on-site spent fuel management and elevated design basis threats.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject, since there are diverging views on this topic, and multibillion-dollar investments ride on the choice of an energy source. Comparison with other power generation methods is strongly dependent on assumptions about construction timescales and capital financing for nuclear plants as well as the future costs of fossil fuels and renewables as well as for energy storage solutions for intermittent power sources. Cost estimates also need to take into account <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">plant decommissioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a> storage costs. On the other hand, measures to <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate</a> <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions trading">carbon emissions trading</a>, may favor the economics of nuclear power.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power_organizations">Nuclear power organizations</span></h3>
<p>There are multiple organizations which have taken a position on nuclear power and the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear power industry">nuclear power industry</a>– some are proponents, and some are opponents.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proponents">Proponents</span></h4>
<p>The majority of pro-nuclear energy organizations and associations is either industry-supported or directly formed from industry members as <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">advocacy groups</a> or <a href="/wiki/Trade_association" title="Trade association">trade associations</a>.</p>

</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_the_industry">Future of the industry</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_prospective_nuclear_units_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="List of prospective nuclear units in the United States">List of prospective nuclear units in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear power in the United States">Nuclear power in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of global warming">Mitigation of global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg/220px-Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg/330px-Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg/440px-Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1090" data-file-height="766" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brunswick-Nuclear-Plant-Discharge-Canal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brunswick Nuclear Plant discharge canal</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/220px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/330px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/440px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Bruce Nuclear Generating Station">Bruce Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_power_stations" title="List of nuclear power stations">largest nuclear power facility</a> in the world<sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-169">[169]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The future of nuclear power varies greatly between countries, depending on government policies. Some countries, many of them in Europe, such as Germany, Belgium, and Lithuania, have adopted policies of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_phase-out" title="Nuclear power phase-out">nuclear power phase-out</a>. At the same time, some Asian countries, such as China,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> South Korea,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> and India,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> have committed to rapid expansion of nuclear power. Many other countries, such as the United Kingdom<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> and the United States, have policies in between. Japan was a major generator of nuclear power before the Fukushima accident, but as of August 2016, Japan has restarted only three of its nuclear plants, and the extent to which it will resume its nuclear program is uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, the International Energy Agency reported that the Fukushima accident had a strongly negative effect on nuclear power, yet “the prospects for nuclear energy remain positive in the medium to long term despite a negative impact in some countries in the aftermath of the accident.” The IEA noted that at the start of 2014, there were 72 nuclear reactors under construction worldwide, the largest number in 25 years, and that China planned to increase nuclear power capacity from 17 gigawatts (GW) in 2014, to 58 GW in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, the US Energy Information Administration projected for its “base case” that world nuclear power generation would increase from 2,344 billion kW-hr in 2012 to 4,501 billion kW-hr in 2040. Most of the predicted increase was expected to be in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>The nuclear power industry in western nations has a history of construction delays, <a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant">cost overruns</a>, plant cancellations, and nuclear safety issues despite <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Power_2010_Program" title="Nuclear Power 2010 Program">significant government subsidies and support</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jk_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jk-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greeninc_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rb_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rb-179">[179]</a></sup> In December 2013, <i>Forbes</i> magazine cited a report which concluded that, in western countries, "reactors are not a viable source of new power".<sup id="cite_ref-jmc_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmc-180">[180]</a></sup> Even where they make economic sense, they are not feasible because nuclear’s "enormous costs, political and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">popular opposition</a>, and regulatory uncertainty".<sup id="cite_ref-jmc_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmc-180">[180]</a></sup> This view echoes the statement of former Exelon CEO <a href="/wiki/John_Rowe_(Exelon)" title="John Rowe (Exelon)">John Rowe</a>, who said in 2012 that new nuclear plants in the United States "don’t make any sense right now" and won’t be economically viable in the foreseeable future.<sup id="cite_ref-jmc_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmc-180">[180]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Quiggin" title="John Quiggin">John Quiggin</a>, economics professor, also says the main problem with the nuclear option is that it is not economically-viable. Quiggin says that we need more <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficient energy use</a> and more <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">renewable energy commercialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Quiggin_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quiggin-152">[152]</a></sup> Former NRC member <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bradford" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Bradford">Peter Bradford</a> and Professor <a href="/wiki/Ian_Lowe" title="Ian Lowe">Ian Lowe</a> made similar statements in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Hannah_Northey_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannah_Northey-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lowenuke_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowenuke-182">[182]</a></sup> However, some "nuclear cheerleaders" and lobbyists in the West continue to champion reactors, often with proposed new but largely untested designs, as a source of new power.<sup id="cite_ref-jmc_180-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jmc-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hannah_Northey_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannah_Northey-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leoh_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leoh-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Diane_Farseta_38.E2.80.9356_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diane_Farseta_38.E2.80.9356-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Nuclear_Charm_Offensive_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Nuclear_Charm_Offensive-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ucsusa.org_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucsusa.org-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-businessweek.com_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek.com-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much more new build activity is occurring in developing countries like South Korea, India and China. In March 2016, China had 30 reactors in operation, 24 under construction and plans to build more,<sup id="cite_ref-WNAChina_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNAChina-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_China_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_China-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> However, according to a government research unit, China must not build "too many nuclear power reactors too quickly", in order to avoid a shortfall of fuel, equipment and qualified plant workers.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the US, licenses of almost half its reactors have been extended to 60&#160;years,<sup id="cite_ref-world-nuclear_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-nuclear-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-matt2010_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matt2010-193">[193]</a></sup> Two new <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> are under construction at <a href="/wiki/Vogtle" class="mw-redirect" title="Vogtle">Vogtle</a>, a dual construction project which marks the end of a 34-year period of stagnation in the US construction of civil nuclear power reactors. The station operator licenses of almost half the present 104 power reactors in the US, as of 2008, have been given <a href="/wiki/Light_Water_Reactor_Sustainability_Program" title="Light Water Reactor Sustainability Program">extensions to 60&#160;years</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-world-nuclear_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-nuclear-192">[192]</a></sup> As of 2012, U.S. nuclear industry officials expect five new reactors to enter service by 2020, all at existing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-us12_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us12-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2013, four aging, uncompetitive, reactors were permanently closed.<sup id="cite_ref-Mark_Cooper_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_Cooper-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mw11111_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw11111-145">[145]</a></sup> Relevant state legislatures are trying to close <a href="/wiki/Vermont_Yankee" class="mw-redirect" title="Vermont Yankee">Vermont Yankee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Nuclear Power Plant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mw11111_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw11111-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. NRC and the U.S. Department of Energy have initiated research into <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor_sustainability" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor sustainability">Light water reactor sustainability</a> which is hoped will lead to allowing extensions of reactor licenses beyond 60 years, provided that safety can be maintained, as the loss in non-CO<sub>2</sub>-emitting generation capacity by retiring reactors "may serve to challenge U.S. energy security, potentially resulting in increased greenhouse gas emissions, and contributing to an imbalance between electric supply and demand."<sup id="cite_ref-LifeAfter60_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LifeAfter60-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a possible impediment to production of nuclear power plants as only a few companies worldwide have the capacity to forge single-piece reactor pressure vessels,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> which are necessary in the most common reactor designs. Utilities across the world are submitting orders years in advance of any actual need for these vessels. Other manufacturers are examining various options, including making the component themselves, or finding ways to make a similar item using alternate methods.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a>, globally during the 1980s one new nuclear reactor started up every 17&#160;days on average, and in the year 2015 it was estimated that this rate could in theory eventually increase to one every 5&#160;days, although no plans exist for that.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> As of 2007, <a href="/wiki/Watts_Bar_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Watts Bar Nuclear Generating Station">Watts Bar 1</a> in Tennessee, which came on-line on February 7, 1996, was the last U.S. commercial nuclear reactor to go on-line. This is often quoted as evidence of a successful worldwide campaign for nuclear power phase-out.<sup id="cite_ref-INL_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INL-198">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electricity_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity shortage">Electricity shortages</a>, fossil fuel price increases, global warming, and heavy metal emissions from fossil fuel use, new technology such as passively safe plants, and national energy security may renew the demand for nuclear power plants.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power_plant">Nuclear power plant</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/220px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png" width="220" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/330px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/440px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png 2x" data-file-width="807" data-file-height="349" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unlike <a href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil fuel power plants</a>, the only substance leaving the cooling towers of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear power plants">nuclear power plants</a> is water vapour and thus does not <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">pollute the air</a> or cause <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plant</a></div>
<p>Just as many conventional <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a> generate electricity by harnessing the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> released from burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>, nuclear power plants convert the energy released from the nucleus of an atom via nuclear fission that takes place in a nuclear reactor. The heat is removed from the reactor core by a cooling system that uses the heat to generate steam, which drives a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> connected to a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">generator</a> producing electricity.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle_of_nuclear_fuel">Life cycle of nuclear fuel</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/220px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/330px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/440px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 2x" data-file-width="923" data-file-height="914" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The nuclear fuel cycle begins when uranium is mined, enriched, and manufactured into nuclear fuel, (1) which is delivered to a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. After usage in the power plant, the spent fuel is delivered to a reprocessing plant (2) or to a final repository (3) for geological disposition. In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a> 95% of spent fuel can potentially be recycled to be returned to usage in a power plant (4).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</a></div>
<p>A nuclear reactor is only part of the life-cycle for nuclear power. The process starts with mining (see <i><a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">Uranium mining</a></i>). Uranium mines are underground, <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open-pit</a>, or <a href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" class="mw-redirect" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> mines. In any case, the uranium ore is extracted, usually converted into a stable and compact form such as <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a>, and then transported to a processing facility. Here, the yellowcake is converted to <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a>, which is then <a href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" class="mw-redirect" title="Uranium enrichment">enriched</a> using various techniques. At this point, the enriched uranium, containing more than the natural 0.7% U-235, is used to make <a href="/wiki/Fuel_rod#Common_physical_forms_of_nuclear_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel rod">rods</a> of the proper composition and geometry for the particular reactor that the fuel is destined for. The fuel rods will spend about 3 operational cycles (typically 6 years total now) inside the reactor, generally until about 3% of their uranium has been fissioned, then they will be moved to a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> where the short lived isotopes generated by fission can decay away. After about 5 years in a spent fuel pool the spent fuel is radioactively and thermally cool enough to handle, and it can be moved to dry storage casks or reprocessed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_fuel_resources">Conventional fuel resources</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">Uranium market</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_development#Nuclear_energy" title="Energy development">Energy development - Nuclear energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/200px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png" width="200" height="584" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/300px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/400px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="742" data-file-height="2165" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Proportions of the isotopes, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (blue) and uranium-235 (red) found naturally, versus grades that are <a href="/wiki/Enriched_Uranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Enriched Uranium">enriched</a>. <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> require fuel enriched to (3-4%), while others such as the <a href="/wiki/CANDU" class="mw-redirect" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactor uses natural uranium.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> is a fairly common <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in the Earth's crust. Uranium is approximately as common as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> in the Earth's crust, and is about 40&#160;times more common than silver.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Uranium is present in trace concentrations in most rocks, dirt, and ocean water, but can be economically extracted currently only where it is present in high concentrations. Still, the world's present measured resources of uranium, economically recoverable at the arbitrary price ceiling of 130&#160;USD/kg, are enough to last for between 70 and 100 years.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Red_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Red-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a> in 2006, there was an expected 85 years worth of uranium in already identified resources, when that uranium is used in <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">present reactor technology</a>, in the OECD's red book of 2011, due to increased exploration, known uranium resources have grown by 12.5% since 2008, with this increase translating into greater than a century of uranium available if the metals usage rate were to continue at the 2011 level.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> The OECD also estimate 670 years of economically recoverable uranium in total conventional resources and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> ores, while also using present reactor technology, a resource that is recoverable from between 60-100 US$/kg of Uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> In a similar manner to every other natural metal resource, for every tenfold increase in the cost per kilogram of uranium, there is a three-hundredfold increase in available lower quality ores that would then become economical.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> As the OECD note:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Even if the nuclear industry expands significantly, sufficient fuel is available for centuries. If advanced <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeder reactors</a> could be designed in the future to efficiently utilize recycled or depleted uranium and all actinides, then the resource utilization efficiency would be further improved by an additional factor of eight.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>For example, the OECD have determined that with a pure <a href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> fuel cycle with a burn up of, and recycling of, all the Uranium and <a href="/wiki/Actinides" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinides">actinides</a>, actinides which presently make up the most hazardous substances in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a>, there is 160,000 years worth of Uranium in total conventional resources and phosphate ore, at the price of 60-100 US$/kg of Uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Current <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> make relatively inefficient use of nuclear fuel, mostly fissioning only the very rare uranium-235 isotope. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a> can make this waste reusable, and more efficient reactor designs, such as the currently under construction <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> achieve a higher efficiency burn up of the available resources, than the current vintage <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">generation II reactors</a>, which make up the vast majority of reactors worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This category needs to be <b>updated</b>. Please update this category to reflect recent events or newly available information. Relevant discussion may be found on <a href="/wiki/Talk:Nuclear_power" title="Talk:Nuclear power">the talk page</a>. <small><i>(October 2015)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a></div>
<p>As opposed to current light water reactors which use uranium-235 (0.7% of all natural uranium), fast breeder reactors use uranium-238 (99.3% of all natural uranium). It has been estimated that there is up to five billion years' worth of uranium-238 for use in these power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford-cohen_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-cohen-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breeder technology has been used in several reactors, but the high cost of reprocessing fuel safely, at 2006 technological levels, requires uranium prices of more than 200 USD/kg before becoming justified economically.<sup id="cite_ref-wna-anpr_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna-anpr-210">[210]</a></sup> Breeder reactors are still however being pursued as they have the potential to burn up all of the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> in the present inventory of nuclear waste while also producing power and creating additional quantities of fuel for more reactors via the breeding process.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> In 2005, there were two breeder reactors producing power: the <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%A9nix" title="Phénix">Phénix</a> in France, which has since powered down in 2009 after 36 years of operation, and the <a href="/wiki/BN-600_reactor" title="BN-600 reactor">BN-600 reactor</a>, a reactor constructed in 1980 Beloyarsk, Russia which is still operational as of 2013. The electricity output of BN-600 is 600 MW — Russia plans to expand the nation's use of breeder reactors with the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800 reactor</a>, was scheduled to become operational in 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> but due to delays is not scheduled to produce power until 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> The technical design of a yet larger breeder, the <a href="/wiki/BN-1200_reactor" title="BN-1200 reactor">BN-1200 reactor</a> was originally scheduled to be finalized in 2013, with construction slated for 2015 but has also been delayed.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Japan's <a href="/wiki/Monju_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Monju Nuclear Power Plant">Monju</a> breeder reactor restarted (having been shut down in 1995) in 2010 for 3 months, but shut down again after equipment fell into the reactor during reactor checkups, it is planned to become re-operational in late 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> Both China and India are building breeder reactors. With the Indian 500 MWe <a href="/wiki/Prototype_Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor">Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor</a> scheduled to become operational in 2014, with plans to build five more by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/China_Experimental_Fast_Reactor" title="China Experimental Fast Reactor">China Experimental Fast Reactor</a> began producing power in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another alternative to fast breeders is thermal breeder reactors that use uranium-233 bred from <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> as fission fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a>. Thorium is about 3.5 times more common than uranium in the Earth's crust, and has different geographic characteristics. This would extend the total practical fissionable resource base by 450%.<sup id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-219">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India's three-stage nuclear power programme">India's three-stage nuclear power programme</a> features the use of a thorium fuel cycle in the third stage, as it has abundant thorium reserves but little uranium.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_waste">Solid waste</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">Radioactive waste</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of nuclear waste treatment technologies">List of nuclear waste treatment technologies</a></div>
<p>The most important waste stream from nuclear power plants is <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>. It is primarily composed of unconverted uranium as well as significant quantities of transuranic actinides (plutonium and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>, mostly). In addition, about 3% of it is fission products from nuclear reactions. The actinides (uranium, plutonium, and curium) are responsible for the bulk of the long-term radioactivity, whereas the fission products are responsible for the bulk of the short-term radioactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level_radioactive_waste">High-level radioactive waste</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">High-level radioactive waste management</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="684" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_rod" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear fuel rod">nuclear fuel rod</a> assembly bundle being inspected before entering a reactor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/220px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/330px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/440px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1139" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Following interim storage in a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a>, the bundles of used fuel assemblies of a typical nuclear power station are often stored on site in the likes of the eight <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels pictured above.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a>, which generated 44 billion <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> of electricity over its lifetime, its complete spent fuel inventory is contained within sixteen casks.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>High-level radioactive waste management concerns management and disposal of highly radioactive materials created during production of nuclear power. The technical issues in accomplishing this are daunting, due to the extremely long periods <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive wastes</a> remain deadly to living organisms. Of particular concern are two <a href="/wiki/Long-lived_fission_product" title="Long-lived fission product">long-lived fission products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99" title="Technetium-99">Technetium-99</a> (half-life 220,000 years) and <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">Iodine-129</a> (half-life 15.7 million years),<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> which dominate spent nuclear fuel radioactivity after a few thousand years. The most troublesome <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranic elements</a> in spent fuel are <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_neptunium" title="Isotopes of neptunium">Neptunium-237</a> (half-life two million years) and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">Plutonium-239</a> (half-life 24,000 years).<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Consequently, high-level radioactive waste requires sophisticated treatment and management to successfully isolate it from the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. This usually necessitates treatment, followed by a long-term management strategy involving permanent storage, disposal or transformation of the waste into a non-toxic form.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Governments around the world are considering a range of waste management and disposal options, usually involving deep-geologic placement, although there has been limited progress toward implementing long-term waste management solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> This is partly because the timeframes in question when dealing with radioactive waste range from 10,000 to millions of years,<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> according to studies based on the effect of estimated radiation doses.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some proposed nuclear reactor designs however such as the American <a href="/wiki/Integral_Fast_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Integral Fast Reactor">Integral Fast Reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">Molten salt reactor</a> can use the nuclear waste from light water reactors as a fuel, transmutating it to isotopes that would be safe after hundreds, instead of tens of thousands of years. This offers a potentially more attractive alternative to deep geological disposal.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another possibility is the use of thorium in a reactor especially designed for thorium (rather than mixing in thorium with uranium and plutonium (i.e. in existing reactors). Used thorium fuel remains only a few hundreds of years radioactive, instead of tens of thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the fraction of a radioisotope's atoms decaying per unit of time is inversely proportional to its half-life, the relative radioactivity of a quantity of buried human radioactive waste would diminish over time compared to natural radioisotopes (such as the decay chains of 120 trillion tons of thorium and 40 trillion tons of uranium which are <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">at relatively trace concentrations of parts per million each</a> over the crust's 3 * 10<sup>19</sup> ton mass).<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> For instance, over a timeframe of thousands of years, after the most active short half-life radioisotopes decayed, burying U.S. nuclear waste would increase the radioactivity in the top 2000 feet of rock and soil in the United States (10 million km<sup>2</sup>) by <a href="/wiki/Approximation#Mathematics" title="Approximation">≈</a> 1 part in 10 million over the cumulative amount of <a href="/wiki/Natural_radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural radioactivity">natural radioisotopes</a> in such a volume, although the vicinity of the site would have a far higher concentration of artificial radioisotopes underground than such an average.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level_radioactive_waste">Low-level radioactive waste</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-level_waste" title="Low-level waste">Low-level waste</a></div>
<p>The nuclear industry also produces a large volume of low-level radioactive waste in the form of contaminated items like clothing, hand tools, water purifier resins, and (upon decommissioning) the materials of which the reactor itself is built. In the US, the Nuclear Regulatory Commission has repeatedly attempted to allow low-level materials to be handled as normal waste: landfilled, recycled into consumer items, etcetera.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparing_radioactive_waste_to_industrial_toxic_waste">Comparing radioactive waste to industrial toxic waste</span></h4>
<p>In countries with nuclear power, radioactive wastes comprise less than 1% of total industrial toxic wastes, much of which remains hazardous for long periods.<sup id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-208">[208]</a></sup> Overall, nuclear power produces far less waste material by volume than fossil-fuel based power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Coal-burning plants are particularly noted for producing large amounts of toxic and mildly radioactive ash due to concentrating naturally occurring metals and mildly radioactive material from the coal.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> A 2008 report from <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> concluded that coal power actually results in more radioactivity being released into the environment than nuclear power operation, and that the population <a href="/wiki/Effective_dose_(radiation_safety)" class="mw-redirect" title="Effective dose (radiation safety)">effective dose</a> equivalent, or dose to the public from radiation from coal plants is 100 times as much as from the operation of nuclear plants.<sup id="cite_ref-colmain_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colmain-240">[240]</a></sup> Indeed, coal ash is much less radioactive than spent nuclear fuel on a weight per weight basis, but coal ash is produced in much higher quantities per unit of energy generated, and this is released directly into the environment as <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, whereas nuclear plants use shielding to protect the environment from radioactive materials, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels.<sup id="cite_ref-cejournal_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cejournal-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span></h4>
<p>Disposal of nuclear waste is often said to be the Achilles' heel of the industry.<sup id="cite_ref-mont2011_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-242">[242]</a></sup> Presently, waste is mainly stored at individual reactor sites and there are over 430 locations around the world where radioactive material continues to accumulate. Some experts suggest that centralized underground repositories which are well-managed, guarded, and monitored, would be a vast improvement.<sup id="cite_ref-mont2011_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-242">[242]</a></sup> There is an "international consensus on the advisability of storing nuclear waste in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repositories</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-go_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-go-243">[243]</a></sup> with the lack of movement of nuclear waste in the 2 billion year old <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> being cited as "a source of essential information today."<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are no commercial scale purpose built underground repositories in operation.<sup id="cite_ref-go_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-go-243">[243]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> (WIPP) in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> has been taking nuclear waste since 1999 from production reactors, but as the name suggests is a research and development facility. A radiation leak at WIPP in 2014 brought renewed attention to the need for R&amp;D on disposal or radioactive waste and spent fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeff_Tollefson_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeff_Tollefson-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprocessing">Reprocessing</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a>.</div>
<p>Reprocessing can potentially recover up to 95% of the remaining uranium and plutonium in spent nuclear fuel, putting it into new <a href="/wiki/Mixed_oxide_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed oxide fuel">mixed oxide fuel</a>. This produces a reduction in long term radioactivity within the remaining waste, since this is largely short-lived fission products, and reduces its volume by over 90%. Reprocessing of civilian fuel from power reactors is currently done in Europe, Russia, Japan, and India. The full potential of reprocessing has not been achieved because it requires breeder reactors, which are not commercially available.<sup id="cite_ref-berrytoll_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-250">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEEE_Spectrum_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_Spectrum-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nuclear reprocessing reduces the volume of high-level waste, but by itself does not reduce radioactivity or heat generation and therefore does not eliminate the need for a geological waste repository. Reprocessing has been politically controversial because of the potential to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, the potential vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a>, the political challenges of repository siting (a problem that applies equally to direct disposal of spent fuel), and because of its high cost compared to the once-through fuel cycle. Several different methods for reprocessing been tried, but many have had safety and practicality problems which have led to their discontinuation.<sup id="cite_ref-berrytoll_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-250">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bas2011_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, the Obama administration stepped back from President Bush's plans for commercial-scale reprocessing and reverted to a program focused on reprocessing-related scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Reprocessing is not allowed in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nature.com_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-255">[255]</a></sup> In the U.S., spent nuclear fuel is currently all treated as waste.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> A major recommendation of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America%27s_Nuclear_Future" title="Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future">Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future</a> was that "the United States should undertake an integrated nuclear waste management program that leads to the timely development of one or more permanent deep geological facilities for the safe disposal of spent fuel and high-level nuclear waste".<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America.E2.80.99s_Nuclear_Future_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America.E2.80.99s_Nuclear_Future-257">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depleted_uranium">Depleted uranium</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">Depleted uranium</a></div>
<p>Uranium enrichment produces many tons of <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a> (DU) which consists of U-238 with most of the easily fissile U-235 isotope removed. U-238 is a tough metal with several commercial uses—for example, aircraft production, radiation shielding, and armor—as it has a higher density than lead. Depleted uranium is also controversially used in munitions; DU penetrators (bullets or <a href="/wiki/APFSDS" class="mw-redirect" title="APFSDS">APFSDS</a> tips) "self sharpen", due to uranium's tendency to fracture along shear bands.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents.2C_attacks_and_safety">Accidents, attacks and safety</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents">Accidents</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/220px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/330px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/440px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1095" data-file-height="612" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, the world's worst <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a> since 1986, displaced 50,000 households after <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans of some shipments of vegetables and fish.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">Energy accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear safety">Nuclear safety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_nuclear_disasters_and_radioactive_incidents" title="Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents">Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents</a></div>
<p>Some serious <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have occurred. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_K._Sovacool" title="Benjamin K. Sovacool">Benjamin K. Sovacool</a> has reported that worldwide there have been 99 accidents at nuclear power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-critev_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critev-262">[262]</a></sup> Fifty-seven accidents have occurred since the Chernobyl disaster, and 57% (56 out of 99) of all nuclear-related accidents have occurred in the USA.<sup id="cite_ref-critev_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critev-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bksaccident_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bksaccident-263">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nuclear power plant accidents include the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> (1986) with approximately 60 deaths so far attributed to the accident and a predicted, eventual total death toll, of from 4000 to 25,000 latent cancers deaths. The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster (2011), has not caused any radiation related deaths, with a predicted, eventual total death toll, of from 0 to 1000, and the Three Mile Island accident (1979), no <a href="/wiki/Causal" class="mw-redirect" title="Causal">causal</a> deaths, cancer or otherwise, have been found in follow up studies of this accident.<sup id="cite_ref-timenuke_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-18">[18]</a></sup> Nuclear-powered submarine mishaps include the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-19" title="Soviet submarine K-19">K-19</a> reactor accident (1961),<sup id="cite_ref-rad_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rad-19">[19]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">K-27</a> reactor accident (1968),<sup id="cite_ref-johnston2007_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-20">[20]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-431" title="Soviet submarine K-431">K-431</a> reactor accident (1985).<sup id="cite_ref-timenuke_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-18">[18]</a></sup> International research is continuing into safety improvements such as passively safe plants,<sup id="cite_ref-David_Baurac_2002_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Baurac_2002-125">[125]</a></sup> and the possible future use of nuclear fusion.</p>
<p>In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, nuclear power has caused fewer accidental deaths per unit of energy generated than all other major sources of energy generation. Energy produced by coal, petroleum, natural gas and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> has caused more deaths per unit of energy generated, from air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>. This is found in the following comparisons, when the immediate nuclear related deaths from accidents are compared to the immediate deaths from these other energy sources,<sup id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-23">[23]</a></sup> when the latent, or predicted, indirect cancer deaths from nuclear <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a> are compared to the immediate deaths from the above energy sources,<sup id="cite_ref-theage2006_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> and when the combined immediate and indirect fatalities from nuclear power and all fossil fuels are compared, fatalities resulting from the mining of the necessary natural resources to power generation and to air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-21">[21]</a></sup> With these data, the use of nuclear power has been calculated to have prevented in the region of 1.8 million deaths between 1971 and 2009, by reducing the proportion of energy that would otherwise have been generated by fossil fuels, and is projected to continue to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-265">[265]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although according to Benjamin K. Sovacool, fission energy accidents ranked first in terms of their total economic cost, accounting for 41 percent of all property damage attributed to energy accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> Analysis presented in the international Journal, <i>Human and Ecological Risk Assessment</i> found that coal, oil, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_petroleum_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid petroleum gas">Liquid petroleum gas</a> and hydroelectric accidents(primarily due to the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_dam" class="mw-redirect" title="Banqiao dam">Banqiao dam</a> burst) have resulted in greater economic impacts than nuclear power accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the 2011 Japanese <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a>, authorities shut down the nation's 54 nuclear power plants, but it has been estimated that if Japan had never adopted nuclear power, accidents and pollution from coal or gas plants would have caused more lost years of life.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> As of 2013, the Fukushima site remains <a href="/wiki/Radiation_effects_from_Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation effects from Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">highly radioactive</a>, with some 160,000 evacuees still living in temporary housing, and some land will be unfarmable for centuries. The difficult <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_disaster_cleanup" title="Fukushima disaster cleanup">Fukushima disaster cleanup</a> will take 40 or more years, and cost tens of billions of dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-Richard_Schiffman_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Schiffman-269">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martin_Fackler_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Fackler-270">[270]</a></sup></p>
<p>Forced evacuation from a nuclear accident may lead to social isolation, anxiety, depression, psychosomatic medical problems, reckless behavior, even suicide. Such was the outcome of the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Chernobyl nuclear disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> in Ukraine.
