A comprehensive 2005 study concluded that "the mental health impact of Chernobyl is the largest public health problem unleashed by the accident to date".<sup id="cite_ref-riv12_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riv12-271">[271]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_N._von_Hippel" title="Frank N. von Hippel">Frank N. von Hippel</a>, a U.S. scientist, commented on the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, saying that "fear of ionizing radiation could have long-term psychological effects on a large portion of the population in the contaminated areas".<sup id="cite_ref-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27.E2.80.9336_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27.E2.80.9336-272">[272]</a></sup> A 2015 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">Lancet</a></i> explained that serious impacts of nuclear accidents were often not directly attributable to radiation exposure, but rather social and psychological effects. Evacuation and long-term displacement of affected populations created problems for many people, especially the elderly and hospital patients.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> But long-term displacement is not a unique feature to nuclear accidents, with hydropower and lignite surface mining projects routinely displacing thousands during normal, non-accident, operations, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> resp. <a href="/wiki/Garzweiler_surface_mine" title="Garzweiler surface mine">Garzweiler surface mine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_and_sabotage">Attacks and sabotage</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack" title="Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack">Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">Nuclear terrorism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear safety in the United States">Nuclear safety in the United States</a></div>
<p>Terrorists could target <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a> in an attempt to release <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> into the community. The United States 9/11 Commission has said that nuclear power plants were potential targets originally considered for the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001">September 11, 2001</a> attacks. An attack on a reactor’s <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> could also be serious, as these pools are less protected than the reactor core. The release of radioactivity could lead to thousands of near-term deaths and greater numbers of long-term fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-fas12_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-274">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>If nuclear power use is to expand significantly, nuclear facilities will have to be made extremely safe from attacks that could release massive quantities of radioactivity into the community. New reactor designs have features of <a href="/wiki/Passive_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive safety">passive safety</a>, such as the flooding of the reactor core without active intervention by reactor operators. But these safety measures have generally been developed and studied with respect to accidents, not to the deliberate reactor attack by a terrorist group. However, the US <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> does now also require new reactor license applications to consider security during the design stage.<sup id="cite_ref-fas12_274-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-274">[274]</a></sup> In the United States, the NRC carries out "Force on Force" (FOF) exercises at all Nuclear Power Plant (NPP) sites at least once every three years.<sup id="cite_ref-fas12_274-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-274">[274]</a></sup> In the U.S., plants are surrounded by a double row of tall fences which are electronically monitored. The plant grounds are patrolled by a sizeable force of armed guards.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>Insider sabotage regularly occurs, because insiders can observe and work around security measures. Successful insider crimes depended on the perpetrators' observation and knowledge of security vulnerabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> A fire caused 5–10 million dollars worth of damage to New York's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Energy Center</a> in 1971. The arsonist turned out to be a plant maintenance worker. Sabotage by workers has been reported at many other reactors in the United States: at <a href="/wiki/Zion_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Zion Nuclear Power Station">Zion Nuclear Power Station</a> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Quad_Cities_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Quad Cities Nuclear Generating Station">Quad Cities Nuclear Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peach_Bottom_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Peach Bottom Nuclear Generating Station">Peach Bottom Nuclear Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_St._Vrain_Generating_Station" title="Fort St. Vrain Generating Station">Fort St. Vrain Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trojan_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Trojan Nuclear Power Plant">Trojan Nuclear Power Plant</a> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Browns_Ferry_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Browns Ferry Nuclear Power Plant">Browns Ferry Nuclear Power Plant</a> (1980), and <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Valley_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Beaver Valley Nuclear Generating Station">Beaver Valley Nuclear Generating Station</a> (1981). Many reactors overseas have also reported sabotage by workers.<sup id="cite_ref-Amory_Lovins_2001_145.E2.80.93146_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amory_Lovins_2001_145.E2.80.93146-277">[277]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</span></h2>
<p>Many technologies and materials associated with the creation of a nuclear power program have a dual-use capability, in that they can be used to make <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> if a country chooses to do so. When this happens a nuclear power program can become a route leading to a nuclear weapon or a public annex to a "secret" weapons program. The concern over <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran" title="Nuclear program of Iran">Iran's nuclear activities</a> is a case in point.<sup id="cite_ref-dfall2009_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-278">[278]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/330px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/440px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="555" data-file-height="405" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United States and <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a>/Russian <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> stockpiles, 1945-2006.The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a> was the main driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-279">[279]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usec.com_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usec.com-280">[280]</a></sup> However without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for <a href="/wiki/Fissile" class="mw-redirect" title="Fissile">fissile</a> fuel, the cost of dismantling has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A fundamental goal for American and global security is to minimize the nuclear proliferation risks associated with the expansion of nuclear power. If this development is "poorly managed or efforts to contain risks are unsuccessful, the nuclear future will be dangerous".<sup id="cite_ref-dfall2009_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-278">[278]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Global_Nuclear_Energy_Partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Nuclear Energy Partnership">Global Nuclear Energy Partnership</a> is one such international effort to create a distribution network in which developing countries in need of energy, would receive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> at a discounted rate, in exchange for that nation agreeing to forgo their own indigenous develop of a uranium enrichment program. The France-based <a href="/wiki/Eurodif" title="Eurodif">Eurodif</a>/<i>European Gaseous Diffusion Uranium Enrichment Consortium</i> was/is one such program that successfully implemented this concept, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Spain" title="Nuclear power in Spain">Spain</a> and other countries without enrichment facilities buying a share of the fuel produced at the French controlled enrichment facility, but without a transfer of technology.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> Iran was an early participant from 1974, and remains a shareholder of Eurodif via <a href="/wiki/Sofidif" class="mw-redirect" title="Sofidif">Sofidif</a>.</p>
<p>According to Benjamin K. Sovacool, a "number of high-ranking officials, even within the United Nations, have argued that they can do little to stop states using nuclear reactors to produce nuclear weapons".<sup id="cite_ref-bks2011_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-282">[282]</a></sup> A 2009 United Nations report said that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>the revival of interest in nuclear power could result in the worldwide dissemination of uranium enrichment and spent fuel reprocessing technologies, which present obvious risks of proliferation as these technologies can produce fissile materials that are directly usable in nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-bks2011_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-282">[282]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, one factor influencing the support of power reactors is due to the appeal that these reactors have at reducing nuclear weapons arsenals through the <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a>, a program which eliminated 425 metric tons of <a href="/wiki/Highly_enriched_uranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Highly enriched uranium">highly enriched uranium</a>(HEU), the equivalent of 17,000 nuclear warheads, by diluting it with <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a> making it equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Low enriched uranium">low enriched uranium</a>(LEU), and thus suitable as nuclear fuel for commercial fission reactors. This is the single most successful <a href="/wiki/Non-proliferation" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-proliferation">non-proliferation</a> program to date.<sup id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png" class="image"><img alt="Usable nuclear energy in ICBM.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png/220px-Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png/330px-Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png/440px-Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png 2x" data-file-width="473" data-file-height="537" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Usable_nuclear_energy_in_ICBM.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Megatons to Megawatts Program, the brainchild of Thomas Neff of <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014-283">[283]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> was hailed as a major success by anti-nuclear weapon advocates as it has largely been the driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_279-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-279">[279]</a></sup> However without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for fissile fuel, the cost of dismantling and down blending has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.</p>
<p>Currently, according to Harvard professor Matthew Bunn: "The Russians are not remotely interested in extending the program beyond 2013. We've managed to set it up in a way that costs them more and profits them less than them just making new low-enriched uranium for reactors from scratch. But there are other ways to set it up that would be very profitable for them and would also serve some of their strategic interests in boosting their nuclear exports."<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup></p>
<p>Up to 2005, the Megatons to Megawatts Program had processed $8 billion of HEU/weapons grade uranium into LEU/reactor grade uranium, with that corresponding to the elimination of 10,000 nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>For approximately two decades, this material generated nearly 10 percent of all the electricity consumed in the United States (about half of all US nuclear electricity generated) with a total of around 7 trillion <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> of electricity produced.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-287">[287]</a></sup> Enough energy to energize the entire United States electric grid for about two years.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014_283-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014-283">[283]</a></sup> In total it is estimated to have cost $17 billion, a "bargain for US ratepayers", with Russia profiting $12 billion from the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-287">[287]</a></sup> Much needed profit for the <a href="/wiki/Rosatom" title="Rosatom">Russian nuclear oversight industry</a>, which after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet economy">Soviet economy</a>, had difficulties paying for the maintenance and security of the Russian Federations highly enriched uranium and warheads.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014_283-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms.2C_2014-283">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2012 there were <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">thirty one countries</a> that have civil nuclear power plants,<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> of which <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">nine have nuclear weapons</a>, with the vast majority of these <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons state">nuclear weapons states</a> having first produced weapons, before commercial fission electricity stations. Moreover, the re-purposing of civilian nuclear industries for military purposes would be a breach of the <a href="/wiki/Non-proliferation_treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-proliferation treaty">Non-proliferation treaty</a>, of which 190 countries adhere to.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/220px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/330px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/440px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 2008 <a href="/wiki/Meta_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Meta analysis">synthesis</a> of 103 studies, published by Benjamin K. Sovacool, estimated that the value of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for nuclear power over the lifecycle of a plant was 66.08&#160;g/kW·h. Comparative results for various <a href="/wiki/Renewable_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable power">renewable power</a> sources were 9–32&#160;g/kW·h.<sup id="cite_ref-sov_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sov-289">[289]</a></sup> A 2012 study by <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> arrived at a different value, with the mean value, depending on which Reactor design was analyzed, ranging from 11 to 25&#160;g/kW·h of <a href="/wiki/Comparisons_of_life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions">total life cycle</a> nuclear power CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_nuclear_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental effects of nuclear power">Environmental effects of nuclear power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comparisons_of_life-cycle_greenhouse_gas_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions">Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle analysis">Life cycle analysis</a> (LCA) of carbon dioxide emissions show nuclear power as comparable to <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources. Emissions from burning fossil fuels are many times higher.<sup id="cite_ref-sov_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sov-289">[289]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the United Nations (<a href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNSCEAR">UNSCEAR</a>), regular nuclear power plant operation including the nuclear fuel cycle causes radioisotope releases into the environment amounting to 0.0002 <a href="/wiki/Millisievert" class="mw-redirect" title="Millisievert">millisieverts</a> (mSv) per year of public exposure as a global average.<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-293">[293]</a></sup> (Such is small compared to variation in natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a>, which averages 2.4&#160;mSv/a globally but frequently varies between 1&#160;mSv/a and 13&#160;mSv/a depending on a person's location as determined by UNSCEAR).<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-293">[293]</a></sup> As of a 2008 report, the remaining legacy of the worst nuclear power plant accident (Chernobyl) is 0.002&#160;mSv/a in global average exposure (a figure which was 0.04&#160;mSv per person averaged over the entire populace of the Northern Hemisphere in the year of the accident in 1986, although far higher among the most affected local populations and recovery workers).<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_293-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-293">[293]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power#Waste_heat" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power §&#160;Waste_heat</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> causing weather extremes such as <a href="/wiki/Heat_waves" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat waves">heat waves</a>, reduced precipitation levels and <a href="/wiki/Droughts" class="mw-redirect" title="Droughts">droughts</a> can have a significant impact on all <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power station</a> infrastructure, including large biomass-electric and fission-electric stations alike, if cooling in these power stations, namely in the <a href="/wiki/Steam_condenser" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam condenser">steam condenser</a> is provided by certain <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> sources.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> While many thermal stations use indirect seawater cooling or <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling towers</a> that in comparison use little to no freshwater, those that were designed to <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat exchange</a> with rivers and lakes, can run into economic problems.</p>
<p>This presently infrequent generic problem may become increasingly significant over time.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> This can force nuclear reactors to be shut down, as happened in France during the 2003 and 2006 heat waves. Nuclear power supply was severely diminished by low river ﬂow rates and droughts, which meant rivers had reached the maximum temperatures for cooling reactors.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> During the heat waves, 17 reactors had to limit output or shut down. 77% of French electricity is produced by nuclear power and in 2009 a similar situation created a 8GW shortage and forced the French government to import electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> Other cases have been reported from Germany, where extreme temperatures have reduced nuclear power production only 9 times due to high temperatures between 1979 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> In particular:</p>
<ul>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Unterweser_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Unterweser Nuclear Power Plant">Unterweser nuclear power plant</a> reduced output by 90% between June and September 2003<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup></li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Isar_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Isar Nuclear Power Plant">Isar nuclear power plant</a> cut production by 60% for 14 days due to excess river temperatures and low stream ﬂow in the river Isar in 2006<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> However the more modern Isar II station did not have to cut production, as unlike its sister station Isar I, Isar II was built with a cooling tower.</li>
</ul>
<p>Similar events have happened elsewhere in Europe during those same hot summers.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_294-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-294">[294]</a></sup> If global warming continues, this disruption is likely to increase or alternatively, station operators could instead retro-fit other means of cooling, like <a href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a>, despite these frequently being large structures and therefore sometimes unpopular with the public.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_renewable_energy">Comparison with renewable energy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">Cost of electricity by source</a></div>
<p>As of 2013, the World Nuclear Association has said "There is unprecedented interest in renewable energy, particularly solar and wind energy, which provide electricity without giving rise to any carbon dioxide emission. Harnessing these for electricity depends on the cost and efficiency of the technology, which is constantly improving, thus reducing costs per peak kilowatt".<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable electricity">Renewable electricity</a> production, from sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, is frequently criticized for being intermittent or <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kloor_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kloor-296">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like nuclear energy, renewable electricity supply, of primarily <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>, in the 20-50+% range has already been implemented in several European systems, albeit in the context of an integrated European grid system.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovins11_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovins11-298">[298]</a></sup> In 2012, the share of electricity generated by all types of renewable sources in Germany was 21.9%, compared to 16.0% for nuclear power after Germany shut down 7-8 of its 18 nuclear reactors in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, the amount of energy produced from renewable energy is expected to exceed that from nuclear power by 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> and Scotland plans to obtain all electricity from renewable energy by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup> The majority of installed renewable energy across the world is in the form of <a href="/wiki/Hydro_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydro power">hydro power</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> has said that if governments were supportive, and the full complement of renewable energy technologies were deployed, renewable energy supply could account for almost 80% of the world's energy use within forty years.<sup id="cite_ref-ipccccc_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-302">[302]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Pachauri" class="mw-redirect" title="Rajendra Pachauri">Rajendra Pachauri</a>, chairman of the IPCC, said the necessary investment in renewables would cost only about 1% of global GDP annually. This approach could contain greenhouse gas levels to less than 450 parts per million, the safe level beyond which climate change becomes catastrophic and irreversible.<sup id="cite_ref-ipccccc_302-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-302">[302]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> published <i>The Net Benefits of Low and No-Carbon Electricity Technologies</i> which states, after performing an energy and emissions cost analysis, that "The net benefits of new nuclear, hydro, and natural gas combined cycle plants far outweigh the net benefits of new wind or solar plants", with the most cost effective low carbon power technology being determined to be nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, analysis in 2015 by Professor and Chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation's building programs in the 1980s, within 10 years nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets.".<sup id="cite_ref-journals.plos.org_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-journals.plos.org-147">[147]</a></sup> In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a href="/wiki/Synfuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Synfuels">synfuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation's already proven decadal rates(in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>(GW/year/$).<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is in contrast to the completely conceptual paper-studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> more costly global investment per year, which has no historical precedent, having never been attempted due to its prohibitive cost,<sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_149-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-150">[150]</a></sup> along with far greater land that would have to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, and the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_149-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-151">[151]</a></sup> As Brook notes the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_148-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">cost of constructing established nuclear power</a> reactor designs has followed an increasing trend due to <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Court_cases" class="mw-redirect" title="Court cases">court cases</a> whereas the <a href="/wiki/LCOE" class="mw-redirect" title="LCOE">levelized cost of electricity</a> is declining for wind power.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> In about 2011, wind power became as inexpensive as natural gas,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and anti-nuclear groups have suggested that in 2010 solar power became cheaper than nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ncwarn_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncwarn-308">[308]</a></sup> Data from the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">EIA</a> in 2011 estimated that in 2016, solar will have a levelized cost of electricity almost twice that of nuclear (21¢/kWh for solar, 11.39¢/kWh for nuclear), and wind somewhat less (9.7¢/kWh).<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup> However, the US <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">EIA</a> has also cautioned that levelized costs of intermittent sources such as wind and solar are not directly comparable to costs of "dispatchable" sources (those that can be adjusted to meet demand), as intermittent sources need costly large-scale back-up power supplies for when the weather changes.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 study by the Global Subsidies Initiative compared global relative <a href="/wiki/Energy_subsidies" title="Energy subsidies">energy subsidies</a>, or government financial aid to different energy sources, with this aid not solely funnelled into <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> but into bribing or <a href="/wiki/Renewable_portfolio_standard" title="Renewable portfolio standard">"incentivizing" utilities to pursue renewable energy systems, over other options</a>. Results show that fossil fuels receive about 1 US cents per kWh of energy they produce, nuclear energy receives 1.7 cents / kWh, renewable energy (excluding hydroelectricity) receives 5.0 cents / kWh and biofuels receive 5.1 cents / kWh in subsidies.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is however no small volume of intensely radioactive spent fuel that needs to be stored or reprocessed with conventional renewable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> A nuclear plant needs to be disassembled and removed. Much of the disassembled nuclear plant needs to be stored as low level nuclear waste for a few decades.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup> However, from a safety stand point, nuclear power, in terms of lives lost per unit of electricity delivered, is comparable to and in some cases, lower than many renewable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Starfelt_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starfelt-315">[315]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_decommissioning">Nuclear decommissioning</span></h2>
<p>The financial costs of every nuclear power plant continues for some time after the facility has finished generating its last useful electricity. Once no longer economically viable, nuclear reactors and uranium enrichment facilities are generally <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">decommissioned</a>, returning the facility and its parts to a safe enough level to be entrusted for other uses, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenfield_status" title="Greenfield status">greenfield status</a>. After a cooling-off period that may last decades, reactor core materials are dismantled and cut into small pieces to be packed in containers for interim storage or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> experiments. The consensus on how to approach the task is one that is relatively inexpensive, but it has the potential to be hazardous to the natural environment as it presents opportunities for human error, accidents or sabotage.<sup id="cite_ref-sov11_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sov11-316">[316]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the USA a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Waste_Policy_Act" title="Nuclear Waste Policy Act">Nuclear Waste Policy Act</a> and Nuclear Decommissioning Trust Fund is legally required, with utilities banking 0.1 to 0.2 cents/kWh during operations to fund future decommissioning. They must report regularly to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">NRC</a> on the status of their decommissioning funds. About 70% of the total estimated cost of decommissioning all US nuclear power reactors has already been collected (on the basis of the average cost of $320 million per reactor-steam turbine unit).<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the U.S. in 2011, there are 13 reactors that had permanently shut down and are in some phase of decommissioning.<sup id="cite_ref-sov11_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sov11-316">[316]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Yankee_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant">Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a> having completed the process in 2006-2007, after ceasing commercial electricity production circa 1992. The majority of the 15 years, was used to allow the station to naturally <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">cool-down</a> on its own, which makes the manual disassembly process both safer and cheaper.Decommissioning at nuclear sites which have experienced a serious accident are the most expensive and time-consuming.</p>
<p>Working under an <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a> framework that limits or structures accident liabilities in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Convention_on_Third_Party_Liability_in_the_Field_of_Nuclear_Energy" title="Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy">Paris convention on nuclear third-party liability</a>, the Brussels supplementary convention, and the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Civil_Liability_for_Nuclear_Damage" title="Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage">Vienna convention on civil liability for nuclear damage</a><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup> and in the U.S. the <a href="/wiki/Price%E2%80%93Anderson_Nuclear_Industries_Indemnity_Act" title="Price–Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act">Price-Anderson Act</a>. It is often argued that this potential shortfall in liability represents an external cost not included in the cost of nuclear electricity; but the cost is small, amounting to about 0.1% of the levelized cost of electricity, according to a CBO study.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup></p>
<p>These beyond-regular-insurance costs for worst-case scenarios are not unique to nuclear power, as <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a> plants are similarly not fully insured against a catastrophic event such as the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam" title="Banqiao Dam">Banqiao Dam</a> disaster, where 11 million people lost their homes and from 30,000 to 200,000 people died, or large <a href="/wiki/Dam_failure" title="Dam failure">dam failures</a> in general. As private insurers base dam insurance premiums on limited scenarios, major disaster insurance in this sector is likewise provided by the state.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_on_nuclear_power">Debate on nuclear power</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-nuclear_movement" title="Pro-nuclear movement">Pro-nuclear movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Anti-nuclear movement</a></div>
<p>The nuclear power debate concerns the controversy<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eleven_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-64">[64]</a></sup> which has surrounded the deployment and use of nuclear fission reactors to generate electricity from nuclear fuel for civilian purposes. The debate about nuclear power peaked during the 1970s and 1980s, when it "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies", in some countries.<sup id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Proponents of nuclear energy contend that nuclear power is a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> and increases <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> by decreasing dependence on imported energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-14">[14]</a></sup> Proponents claim that nuclear power produces virtually no conventional air pollution, such as greenhouse gases and smog, in contrast to the chief viable alternative of <a href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil fuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup> Nuclear power can produce <a href="/wiki/Base-load" class="mw-redirect" title="Base-load">base-load</a> power unlike many renewables which are <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_energy_source" title="Intermittent energy source">intermittent energy sources</a> lacking large-scale and cheap ways of storing energy.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a> saw oil as a resource that would <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">run out</a>, and proposed nuclear energy as a replacement energy source.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> Proponents claim that the risks of storing waste are small and can be further reduced by using the latest technology in newer reactors, and the operational safety record in the Western world is excellent when compared to the other major kinds of power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup></p>
<p>Opponents believe that nuclear power poses many threats to people and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Share_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Share-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NC_WARN_.C2.BB_Nuclear_Power_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC_WARN_.C2.BB_Nuclear_Power-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sturgis_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-17">[17]</a></sup> These threats include the problems of processing, transport and storage of radioactive nuclear waste, the risk of nuclear weapons proliferation and terrorism, as well as health risks and environmental damage from uranium mining.<sup id="cite_ref-gierec_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gierec-327">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-protest_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-protest-328">[328]</a></sup> They also contend that reactors themselves are enormously complex machines where many things can and do go wrong; and there have been serious <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear accidents">nuclear accidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bksenpol_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bksenpol-329">[329]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup> Critics do not believe that the risks of using nuclear fission as a power source can be fully offset through the development of new technology. They also argue that when all the energy-intensive stages of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear fuel chain">nuclear fuel chain</a> are considered, from uranium mining to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a>, nuclear power is neither a low-carbon nor an economical electricity source.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-markd_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-markd-333">[333]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arguments of <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">economics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear safety">safety</a> are used by both sides of the debate.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_space">Use in space</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_space" title="Nuclear power in space">Nuclear power in space</a></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> and fusion appear promising for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">space propulsion</a> applications, generating higher mission velocities with less <a href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a>. This is due to the much higher energy density of nuclear reactions: some 7 orders of magnitude (10,000,000 times) more energetic than the chemical reactions which power the current generation of rockets.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">Radioactive decay</a> has been used on a relatively small scale (few kW), mostly to power <a href="/wiki/Space_mission" class="mw-redirect" title="Space mission">space missions</a> and experiments by using <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> such as those developed at <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory#Space_and_Security_Power_Systems_Facility" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_concepts">Advanced concepts</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactor</a></div>
<p>Current fission reactors in operation around the world are second or third generation systems, with most of the first-generation systems having been retired some time ago. Research into advanced generation IV reactor types was officially started by the Generation IV International Forum (GIF) based on eight technology goals, including to improve nuclear safety, improve proliferation resistance, minimize waste, improve natural resource utilization, the ability to consume existing nuclear waste in the production of electricity, and decrease the cost to build and run such plants. Most of these reactors differ significantly from current operating light water reactors, and are generally not expected to be available for commercial construction before 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup></p>
<p>The nuclear reactors to be built at Vogtle are new <a href="/wiki/AP1000" title="AP1000">AP1000</a> third generation reactors, which are said to have safety improvements over older power reactors.<sup id="cite_ref-yah12_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yah12-112">[112]</a></sup> However, John Ma, a senior structural engineer at the NRC, is concerned that some parts of the AP1000 steel skin are so brittle that the "impact energy" from a plane strike or storm driven projectile could shatter the wall.<sup id="cite_ref-bs11_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bs11-335">[335]</a></sup> Edwin Lyman, a senior staff scientist at the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a>, is concerned about the strength of the steel containment vessel and the concrete shield building around the AP1000.<sup id="cite_ref-bs11_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bs11-335">[335]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mlw_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlw-336">[336]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Union of Concerned Scientists has referred to the <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR (nuclear reactor)</a>, currently under construction in China, Finland and France, as the only new reactor design under consideration in the United States that "...appears to have the potential to be significantly safer and more secure against attack than today's reactors."<sup id="cite_ref-NPIAWW_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPIAWW-337">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>One disadvantage of any new reactor technology is that safety risks may be greater initially as reactor operators have little experience with the new design. Nuclear engineer <a href="/wiki/David_Lochbaum" title="David Lochbaum">David Lochbaum</a> has explained that almost all serious nuclear accidents have occurred with what was at the time the most recent technology. He argues that "the problem with new reactors and accidents is twofold: scenarios arise that are impossible to plan for in simulations; and humans make mistakes".<sup id="cite_ref-safe_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safe-338">[338]</a></sup> As one director of a U.S. research laboratory put it, "fabrication, construction, operation, and maintenance of new reactors will face a steep learning curve: advanced technologies will have a heightened risk of accidents and mistakes. The technology may be proven, but people are not".<sup id="cite_ref-safe_338-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safe-338">[338]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_nuclear_fusion-fission">Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hybrid_nuclear_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybrid nuclear power">Hybrid nuclear power</a> is a proposed means of generating power by use of a combination of nuclear fusion and fission processes. The concept dates to the 1950s, and was briefly advocated by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to delays in the realization of pure fusion. When a sustained nuclear fusion power plant is built, it has the potential to be capable of extracting all the fission energy that remains in spent fission fuel, reducing the volume of nuclear waste by orders of magnitude, and more importantly, eliminating all actinides present in the spent fuel, substances which cause security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-hybrid_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-339">[339]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion">Nuclear fusion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion power</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Nuclear fusion</a> reactions have the potential to be safer and generate less radioactive waste than fission.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WorldEnergyCouncil_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEnergyCouncil-341">[341]</a></sup> These reactions appear potentially viable, though technically quite difficult and have yet to be created on a scale that could be used in a functional power plant. Fusion power has been under theoretical and experimental investigation since the 1950s.</p>
<p>Construction of the <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> facility began in 2007, but the project has run into many delays and budget overruns. The facility is now not expected to begin operations until the year 2027 – 11 years after initially anticipated.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup> A follow on commercial nuclear fusion power station, <a href="/wiki/DEMO" title="DEMO">DEMO</a>, has been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-ITERorg_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EFDA_Activities_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFDA_Activities-343">[343]</a></sup> There are also suggestions for a power plant based upon a different fusion approach, that of an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_fusion_power_plant" title="Inertial fusion power plant">inertial fusion power plant</a>.</p>
<p>Fusion powered electricity generation was initially believed to be readily achievable, as fission-electric power had been. However, the extreme requirements for continuous reactions and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_containment" title="Plasma containment">plasma containment</a> led to projections being extended by several decades. In 2010, more than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial power production was still believed to be unlikely before 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-ITERorg_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-137">[137]</a></sup></p>

Comparing Nuclear's <i>latent</i> cancer deaths, such as cancer with other energy sources <i>immediate</i> deaths per unit of energy generated(GWeyr). <li><cite class="citation book">Cravens, Gwyneth (2007). <i>Power to Save the World: the Truth about Nuclear Energy</i>. New York: Knopf. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-307-26656-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-307-26656-7">0-307-26656-7</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuclear+power&amp;rft.aufirst=Gwyneth&amp;rft.aulast=Cravens&amp;rft.btitle=Power+to+Save+the+World%3A+the+Truth+about+Nuclear+Energy&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-307-26656-7&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Knopf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>

<li>Ferguson, Charles D., (2007). <i>Nuclear Energy: Balancing Benefits and Risks</i> <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a>.</li>
<li>Garwin, Richard L. and Charpak, Georges (2001) <a href="/wiki/Megawatts_and_Megatons" title="Megawatts and Megatons">Megawatts and Megatons</a> A Turning Point in the Nuclear Age?, Knopf.</li>
<li>Herbst, Alan M. and George W. Hopley (2007). <i>Nuclear Energy Now: Why the Time has come for the World's Most Misunderstood Energy Source</i>, Wiley.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mycle_Schneider" title="Mycle Schneider">Schneider, Mycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Thomas_(professor)" title="Stephen Thomas (professor)">Steve Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antony_Froggatt" title="Antony Froggatt">Antony Froggatt</a>, Doug Koplow (2016). <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Nuclear_Industry_Status_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="The World Nuclear Industry Status Report">The World Nuclear Industry Status Report</a>: World Nuclear Industry Status as of 1 January 2016</i>.</li>
